Shuf'ah in Settlements: Denial vs. Acknowledgment
Ruling statement with textual correction and causal reasoning (talil).

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on shuf'ah in sulh with inkar and iqrar and explain the reasoning behind the distinction.

Core Idea
Shuf'ah is tied to the nature of the settlement: denial shields the sale, acknowledgment triggers pre-emption.

In Hanafi law, the right of pre-emption (shuf'ah) arises from a sale. However, when a property dispute is settled through sulh (compromise), the financial transfer may not always constitute a sale. This passage delineates when such a settlement gives rise to shuf'ah, distinguishing between the defendant's denial (inkar) and acknowledgment (iqrar) of the claimant's right. Understanding this nuance is crucial for correctly applying the rules of pre-emption.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Act and Its Ruling
The act is a settlement (sulh) regarding a disputed property. The ruling governs whether shuf'ah is established. Two scenarios: settlement with denial vs. with acknowledgment.
2
Distinguish the Two Scenarios
With denial (inkar): the defendant does not admit the claimant's ownership; he pays money to avoid an oath or harassment. This payment is not considered a purchase, so no shuf'ah. With acknowledgment (iqrar): the defendant admits the claimant's right, and the settlement is treated as a purchase of the property, thus shuf'ah applies.
Silence is analogous to denial.
3
Examine the Textual Correction
The author notes a correction in al-Mukhtasar: the phrase should be 'salaha 'anha bi-inkar' (settlement about it with denial) instead of 'salaha 'alayha bi-inkar' (settlement upon it with denial). The correction affects the legal implication.
Linguistic precision ensures correct legal reasoning.
4
Understand the Rationale (Talil)
The reasoning (talil) is that with denial, the property remains in the defendant's possession and he claims it is still his; the payment is not for the property but to end the dispute. With acknowledgment, the payment is explicitly for acquiring the ownership, hence a sale.
This is causation (talil) based on intent.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
انکار کے ساتھ مصالحت کرے یا اقرار کے ساتھ ، شفعہ کا حق کس میں حاصل ہوتا ہے؟
قال او يصالح عليها بانكار فان صالح عليها باقرار وجبت الشفعة قال رضى الله عنه هكذا ذكر في اكثر نسخ المختصر و الصحيح او يصالح عنها بانكار مكان قوله عليها لانه اذا صالح عنها بانكار بقى الدار في يده فهو يزعم انها لم تزل عن ملكه وكذا اذا صالح عنها بسكوت لانه يحتمل انه بذل المال افتداء ليمينه وقطعا لشغب خصمه كما اذا انكر صريحا بخلاف ما اذا صالح عنها باقرار لانه معترف بالملك للمدعى وانما استفاده بالصلح فكان مبالة مالية اما اذا صالح عليها باقرار اوسكوت او انكار وجبت الشفعة في جميع ذالك لانه اخذها عوضا عن حقه في زعمه اذا لم يكن من جنسه فيعامل بزعمه. ترجمہ نام قدوری نے کہایا اس نے گھر کے متعلق مصالحت کی ہو انکار کے ساتھ ۔ پس اگر گھر کے متعلق اقرار کے ساتھ مصالحت کرے تو شفعہ ثابت ہو گا۔ حضرت مصنف فرماتے ہیں کہ قدوری کے اکثر نسخوں میں ایسے ہی مذکور ہے اور صحیح بصالح عنہا با نکار ہے ۔ اس کے قول علیہا کی جگہ اسلئے کہ جب اس نے گھر کے بارے میں انکار کے ساتھ مصالحت کی تو گھر اس کے قبضہ میں باقی رہا تو وہ یہ گمان کرتا ہے کہ مکان اس کی ملکیت سے زائل نہیں ہوا اور ایسے ہی جبکہ اس نے سکوت کے ساتھ گھر کے بارے میں مصالحت کی ۔ اسلئے کہ احتمال ہے کہ اس نے مال کو خرچ کیا ہو اپنی یمین کے فدیہ میں اور اپنے خصم کے شور و شعب کو ختم کرنے کیلئے جیسا کہ جبکہ اس نے صراحتا انکار کر دیا ہو۔ بخلاف اس صورت کے جبکہ اس نے گھر کے بارے میں اقرار کے ساتھ مصالحت کی ہو۔ اسلئے کہ وہ مدعی کیلئے ملکیت کا معترف ہے اور
Shall he make a settlement with denial or with acknowledgment? In which is the right of shuf'ah obtained? He said: Or he makes a settlement [upon] it with denial. If he makes a settlement upon it with acknowledgment, then shuf'ah is established. [The author] said, may Allah be pleased with him: Thus it is mentioned in most copies of al-Mukhtasar. And the correct [expression] is: 'or he makes a settlement about it with denial' instead of his saying 'upon it', because when he makes a settlement about it with denial, the house remains in his hand, and he claims that it has not left his ownership. Similarly, if he makes a settlement about it with silence, because it is probable that he paid money as a ransom for his oath and to cut off his opponent's clamor, as when he explicitly denies. Unlike when he makes a settlement upon it with acknowledgment, because he acknowledges the claimant's ownership, and he only acquired it through the settlement, so it is a financial exchange. But if he makes a settlement upon it with acknowledgment, or silence, or denial, shuf'ah is established in all of that, because he takes it as compensation for his right in his claim, if it is not of its type, so it is treated according to his claim. Translation: Nam Quduri said: He made a settlement regarding the house with denial. So if he made a settlement regarding the house with acknowledgment, then shuf'ah is established. The author, may Allah be pleased with him, says: Thus it is mentioned in most copies of al-Mukhtasar. And the correct [expression] is 'he makes a settlement about it with denial' instead of his saying 'upon it', because when he makes a settlement about the house with denial, the house remains in his possession, and he supposes that the house has not left his ownership. And similarly when he makes a settlement about the house with silence, because it is probable that he spent money as a ransom for his oath and to terminate the disturbance of his opponent, as when he explicitly denies. Contrary to the case when he makes a settlement about the house with acknowledgment, because he is acknowledging the claimant's ownership.
Source pages p. 92

Commentary
This passage rules on the application of shuf'ah when a person enters into a sulh (settlement) regarding a property. The key distinction is whether the settlement is made with denial (inkar) or acknowledgment (iqrar) of the claimant's right. According to the Hanafi school, if the settlement is made with denial, the person handing over money is not considered to have purchased the property; rather, he is paying to avoid litigation or oath. Hence, no shuf'ah is established. However, if the settlement is made with acknowledgment, the settlement is treated as a purchase, and shuf'ah applies. The text also notes a textual correction by the author: the correct phrasing in al-Mukhtasar should be 'salaha 'anha bi-inkar' (settlement about it with denial) instead of 'salaha 'alayha bi-inkar' (settlement upon it with denial). The ruling extends to silence as well, which is treated like denial. The concluding part (which seems cut off) mentions that if the settlement is made with acknowledgment, silence, or denial, shuf'ah is established in all cases if the property is taken as compensation for a right and it is not of the same type—then the claim is acted upon.

Key Terms
المصالح (al-musalahah): Settlement; a contract of compromise where one party pays something to the other to end a dispute.
الانكار (al-inkar): Denial; when the defendant denies the claimant's right in the property.
الاقرار (al-iqrar): Acknowledgment; when the defendant admits the claimant's right.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, if a defendant makes a settlement with denial (inkar), why is shuf'ah not established?
A) Because denial invalidates the contract entirely.
B) Because the payment is considered a ransom to avoid an oath, not a purchase price.
C) Because shuf'ah is only established in court-ordered sales.
D) Because the property remains with the defendant and he refuses to sell.

Key Takeaway
Shuf'ah is established in a sulh only when the defendant acknowledges the claimant's right, making the settlement a sale; with denial or silence, it is not a sale and shuf'ah does not apply.

Shuf'ah in Cases of Compromise (Sulh)
Explaining shuf'ah ruling in compromise based on the nature of the settlement

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on shuf'ah in sulh and distinguish between cases where the defendant acknowledges, denies, or remains silent regarding ownership.

Core Idea
In sulh, shuf'ah arises only when the payment is effectively a sale, not when it is merely to end a dispute.

This passage from Ashraf al-Hidayah explores a nuanced area of preemption: when a property dispute is settled through compromise (sulh), does a third party’s right of shuf'ah (preemption) arise? The answer depends on the nature of the settlement. The author breaks down several scenarios based on what the defendant (the person in possession) says during the compromise. Understanding these distinctions is essential for correctly applying shuf'ah in real-world settlements.

Guided Path
1
Classify the compromise scenario
Identify whether the defendant acknowledged, denied, or remained silent about the claimant's ownership during the sulh.
This determines the legal nature of the compromise.
2
Determine if a sale-like exchange occurred
Acknowledgment means the defendant bought the property; denial or silence means the payment was to avoid dispute, not a sale.
Hanafi fiqh focuses on the mutual intent of the parties.
3
Apply the shuf'ah rule
If a sale is established, shuf'ah applies; otherwise, it does not.
4
Consider the textual nuance
Note the author's correction about the preposition in Quduri's matn, which affects whether the house itself is given or something else.
This shows precision in legal drafting.

Source Text

عدنان نے مدعی کی ملکیت کا انکار کیا مگر پھر بھی جھگڑے کا شر دور کرنے کیلئے ہزار روپے دے کر مصالحت کر لی ہو تو اب شفعہ ثابت نہ ہوگا۔ اسلئے کہ عدنان یوں کہتا ہے کہ اس گھر کا برابر میں ہی مالک چلا آرہا ہوں تو بیع کے معنی نہیں پائے گئے ۔ - عدنان نے اس کی ملکیت کا نہ اقرار کیا اور نہ انکار کیا تو اب بھی شفعہ نہ ہو گا۔ اسلئے کہ اس میں احتمال ہے کہ اس نے ہزار روپے اسلئے دیئے ہوں کہ اگر مدعی گواہ پیش نہ کر سکا تو مجھے قسم کھانی پڑے گی اور شریف لوگ حتی الوسع قسم کھانے سے گریز کیا کرتے ہیں۔ اگر چہ سچی ہی قسم کیوں نہ ہو۔ اور یہ بھی احتمال ہے کہ اس نے جھگڑا ختم کرنے کیلئے ہزار روپے دے دیئے ہوں۔ کیونکہ شریف لوگ جھگڑے سے حتی الوسع گریز کیا کرتے ہیں۔ بہر حال اس کے ہزار روپے دے کر مصالحت کرنا اس بات کی دلیل نہیں ہے کہ اس نے مدعی کی ملکیت کا اقرار کر لیا تو یہ انکار کے درجہ میں رہے گا اور شفعہ ثابت نہ ہوگا ۔ مسئلہ پورا ہو گیا۔ دوسرا مسئلہ زید اور عمرو کا کسی چیز کے متعلق جھگڑا ہے۔ زید کہتا ہے میری چیز ہے اور عمر و اس پر قابض ہے اب عمرو نے زید سے مصالحت کر لی اور مصالحت میں زید کو اپنا گھر دے دیا تو اب اس گھر میں شفعہ ہوگا یا نہیں ؟ تو اس کا جواب دیا کہ یہاں پر دو صورتیں ہیں۔ جھگڑا کسی اور چیز کے بارے میں ہے اور مصالحت گھر دے کر کی ہو تو ظاہر ہے کہ یہ گھر زید کے حق کا عوض ہے تو مبادلہ مالی پایا گیا لہذا شفعہ ثابت ہوگا۔ -۲- اس گھر کے بارے میں جھگڑا ہے تو عمرو نے اس کو وہ گھر دے کر مصالحت کر لی تو اب بیع کے معنی نہیں پائے گئے ۔ لہذا شفعہ نہ ہوگا۔ حاصل کلام یہاں شفعہ کے ثبوت کا مدار زید کے گمان پر ہے اگر وہ مکان کو اپنے حق کی جنس میں سے نہ سمجھے تو شفعہ ہے ورنہ نہیں ہے۔ دوسرا مسئلہ ختم ہوا۔ تنبیہ صالح عنها کا مطلب یہ ہوتا ہے کہ گھر کے بارے میں جھگڑا ہے اور کوئی اور چیز دے کر مصالحت کرلی ہو اور بصالح عليها کا مطلب یہ ہے کہ گھر ہی مصالحت میں دیا ہو تو اس پر صاحب ہدایہ نے کہا کہ قدوری کے اکثر نسخوں میں عبارت او يصالح علیھا ہے۔ حالانکہ او يصالح عنھا ہونی چاہئے ورنہ مسئلہ کی نوعیت بدل جائے گی جیسا کہ ظاہر ہے۔
He acquired this property through compromise, so it became a financial exchange. In any case, when he made a compromise by giving a house with acknowledgment, silence, or denial, in all these cases shuf'ah is established. Because he took the house in exchange for his right according to his belief, when it is not of the same type as the right, then the transaction will be based on his belief. Explanation: Muti' al-Rahman filed a claim against Adnan saying that the house in which Adnan resides is mine, and he is occupying it wrongfully. Adnan, to avoid dispute, made a compromise by giving one thousand rupees to Muti' al-Rahman, and both agreed, and the dispute ended. Now Nazim claimed shuf'ah in that house in which Adnan is and regarding which he made compromise by giving one thousand rupees. Question: Now the question is whether Nazim will get shuf'ah or not? Answer: Here the answer is given. The summary is that it depends on the nature of the compromise, which has three forms: 1. Adnan acknowledged that yes the house belongs to Muti' al-Rahman and then made compromise for one thousand rupees, then in this case Nazim will get shuf'ah. Because this is an open sale, and it is clear that the property was of the claimant. The defendant acquired the property in exchange for one thousand rupees. 2. Adnan denied the claimant's ownership but still made compromise by giving one thousand rupees to remove the evil of dispute, then shuf'ah is not established. Because Adnan says that I have always been the owner of this house equally, so the meaning of sale is not found. 3. Adnan neither acknowledged nor denied his ownership, then also shuf'ah is not established. Because there is a possibility that he gave one thousand rupees because if the claimant could not produce witnesses, I would have to take an oath, and noble people avoid taking oaths as much as possible, even if it is a true oath. And there is also a possibility that he gave one thousand rupees to end the dispute, because noble people avoid disputes as much as possible. In any case, his making compromise by giving one thousand rupees is not proof that he acknowledged the claimant's ownership, so it will remain in the category of denial and shuf'ah is not established. The issue is complete. Second issue: Zaid and Amr have a dispute about something. Zaid says it is my thing, and Amr is in possession of it. Now Amr made a compromise with Zaid and gave his house to Zaid in the compromise. So will there be shuf'ah in that house or not? The answer is given that here there are two forms. 1. The dispute is about something else and the compromise was made by giving the house, then it is apparent that this house is the exchange for Zaid's right, so a financial exchange is found, hence shuf'ah is established. 2. The dispute is about this house itself, and Amr made compromise by giving that same house, then the meaning of sale is not found, so shuf'ah is not established. The conclusion is that here the establishment of shuf'ah depends on Zaid's belief; if he does not consider the house to be of the same type as his right, then there is shuf'ah, otherwise not. The second issue is complete. Note: The meaning of 'saliha 'anha' is that the dispute is about a house and compromise was made by giving something else, and the meaning of 'bi saliha 'alaiha' is that the house itself was given in compromise. On this, the author of Hidayah said that in most copies of Quduri the phrase is 'aw yusalihu 'alaiha', whereas it should be 'aw yusalihu 'anha', otherwise the nature of the issue will change, as is apparent.
Source pages p. 93

Commentary
This passage from Ashraf al-Hidayah explains the ruling of shuf'ah (preemption) in cases of sulh (compromise). The author first establishes a general principle: when a person acquires property through compromise (sulh), it is considered a financial exchange, and thus shuf'ah is generally established. However, the crucial factor is the intent and circumstances of the compromise. The text then provides a detailed example: Muti' al-Rahman claims a house occupied by Adnan. Adnan pays 1,000 rupees to settle. A third party, Nazim, now claims shuf'ah in the house. The ruling depends on Adnan's stance during the compromise: (1) If Adnan acknowledged the claimant's ownership, the compromise is effectively a sale, so shuf'ah is established. (2) If Adnan denied ownership, the payment is not for purchase but to end the dispute, so no shuf'ah. (3) If Adnan neither acknowledged nor denied, the payment is ambiguous; since noble people avoid oaths and disputes, the payment is not evidence of a sale, so shuf'ah is not established. The second scenario involves Zaid and Amr. If the dispute is about something else and Amr gives his house as compensation, it is an exchange, and shuf'ah applies. But if the dispute is about that very house, giving it back is not a sale, so no shuf'ah. A concluding note clarifies a textual issue in Quduri's matn regarding the prepositions 'an and 'ala in the phrase for compromise, emphasizing that the correct wording affects the ruling.

Key Terms
صلح (sulh): A compromise to settle a dispute by mutual agreement, often involving payment of money or property.
شفعة (shuf'ah): The right of a co-owner or neighbor to preempt a sale and acquire the property on the same terms.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In a dispute over a house, the defendant pays money to the claimant without acknowledging ownership. According to the Hanafi ruling in this passage, does the right of shuf'ah arise for a third party?
A) Yes, because payment was made in a compromise.
B) No, because the payment was to avoid an oath or end the dispute, not to purchase the house.
C) Yes, if the payment is more than the market value.
D) No, because shuf'ah only applies in formal sales.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi law, shuf'ah in a compromise (sulh) is established only when the compromise functions as a sale, i.e., the defendant acknowledges the claimant's ownership and pays for the property.

Shuf'ah in Gifts: When a Gift Becomes a Sale
Explaining shuf'ah in gifts and when a gift is treated as a sale

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state when shuf'ah applies in a gift and apply the conditions of delivery and division.

Core Idea
A gift with conditioned compensation is treated as a sale for shuf'ah but retains gift conditions.

In Hanafi fiqh, shuf'ah (pre-emption) is generally only established in sales. But what about gifts? This passage clarifies that a pure gift gives no shuf'ah, while a gift conditioned on the donee giving something in return (hibah bi shart al-'iwad) carries the ruling of a sale for shuf'ah purposes. However, such a contract is not entirely a sale: it begins as a gift, so its conditions (delivery and division of shares) must still be met. We will explore this dual nature and the key distinctions.

Guided Path
1
Identify the contract type
First, classify the transaction: is it a pure gift (hibah mutlaqah) or a gift conditioned on compensation (hibah bi shart al-'iwad)? The ruling on shuf'ah hinges on this distinction.
Pure gift = no shuf'ah; conditioned gift = shuf'ah applies.
2
Understand the legal reasoning (qiyas)
The passage argues that a conditioned gift is a sale in its end (bi‘ in intiha'), so shuf'ah follows. Trace the qiyas: the asl (original case) is a sale where shuf'ah is established; the far' (new case) is hibah bi shart al-'iwad; the illa (effective cause) is the exchange of property for compensation, which is the essence of sale.
This is a qiyas jali (clear analogy).
3
Apply gift conditions
Even though shuf'ah applies, the contract begins as a gift. Therefore, the conditions of a valid gift must be present: delivery (qabd) of both the gifted item and the compensation, and both must be divided (not undivided/shuyu').
If these are missing, the contract may be invalid or shuf'ah not established.
4
Contrast with unconditional gift + later payment
If a gift is made unconditionally, and later the donee voluntarily gives compensation, it remains a pure gift. Shuf'ah does not apply, but the compensation prevents the donor from revoking the gift.
The condition must be stipulated in the contract itself.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

چکے ہیں ۔ مگر یہ کہ ہبہ مشروط عوض کے ساتھ ہو۔ اسلئے کہ یہ انتہا کے اعتبار سے بیج ہے اور ضروری ہے قبضہ کا ہونا اور یہ کہ موہوب اور اس کا عوض شائع (غیر مقسوم ) اسلئے کہ یہ ابتداء کے اعتبار سے ہبہ ہے اور کے اعتبار سے ہبہ ہے اور نتہا کے اعتبار سے بیج ہے اور اس کے اندر دونوں کا لحاظ کیا جائے گا یعنی مبدے کا بھی اور منتہا کا بھی ۔ لہذا جب یہ مبدے کے اعتبار سے ہند ہوا تو اس پر ہبہ کے احکام جاری ہوں گے۔ اور ہبہ کے اندر قبضہ شرط ہے اور یہ شرط ہے کہ موہوب اور اس کا عوض دونوں قسمت شدہ ہوں شائع نہ ہوں۔ لہذا ان دونوں شرطوں کا یہاں بھی پایا جانا ضروری ہے اور چونکہ یہ منتہا کے اعتبار سے بیج ہے لہذا اس پر موہوب لڑ نے واہب کو اس کا عوض دے دیا تو اب یہ مطلق ہبہ ہے اور اس کے اندر شفعہ جاری نہ ہوگا نہ موہوب میں اور نہ عوض میں کیونکہ یہ دونوں تھے تو ہبہ ہی، مگر اتنی بات ہے کہ موہوب لڑنے اس کا عوض دے دیا تا کہ واہب رجوع نہ کر سکے۔ اس لئے کہ عوض دینے کی صورت میں ہبہ کے اندر رجوع باطل ہو جاتا ہے۔ تفصیل کیلئے دیکھئے ہدایہ ص ۲۷۳ ج ۳۔
Is there a right of shuf'ah in gifted land? He said: And there is no shuf'ah in a gift for the reason we mentioned, unless it is with a condition of compensation, because it is a sale in its end. And delivery (qabd) is necessary, and that the gifted item and its compensation are not undivided, because it is a gift in the beginning. And we have established this in the Book of Gifts. Contrary to the case when the compensation is not conditioned in the contract, because each of them is an absolute gift, but it is confirmed from it, so revocation is prevented. Translation: Imam Quduri said: There is no shuf'ah in a gift for the reason we mentioned, unless it is a gift with conditioned compensation. Because it is a sale in its end, and delivery is necessary, and that the gifted item and its compensation are not undivided (ghayr maqsum), because it is a gift in the beginning, and we have explained this in the Book of Gifts. Contrary to the case when the compensation is not conditioned in the contract, because each of them is an absolute gift, but the recipient gave compensation for the house, so revocation is prevented. Explanation: Since in a gift the meaning of financial exchange and sale is not found, therefore if someone gives his house as a gift to Zaid, no one will get shuf'ah. However, if it is a gift with condition of compensation (hibah bi shart al-'iwad), then shuf'ah will be given in it. For example, Zaid gives his house as a gift to Khalid on the condition that Khalid will give one thousand rupees as a gift to Zaid. So this is a gift with condition of compensation, and it will be given the status of both gift and sale. That is, it will be said that this contract is a gift in its beginning and a sale in its end, and both aspects will be considered—the beginning and the end. So when it is a gift in the beginning, the rulings of gift will apply. And in a gift, delivery is a condition, and it is a condition that both the gifted item and its compensation be divided, not undivided. Therefore, both these conditions must also be present here. And since it is a sale in its end, the ruling of sale will apply, and shuf'ah is established. The author of Hidayah says that we have explained this in the Book of Gifts (vol. 3, p. 275). Yes, if a gift was made but the condition of compensation was not stipulated in the contract, and later the donee gave compensation to the donor, then this is an absolute gift, and shuf'ah will not apply in it, neither in the gifted item nor in the compensation, because both were gifts. However, the point is that the donee gave compensation so that the donor cannot revoke. Because in the case of giving compensation, revocation in a gift becomes invalid. For details, see Hidayah p. 273 vol. 3.
Source pages p. 94

Commentary
This passage addresses shuf'ah in the context of hibah (gift). The core ruling is that a pure gift (hibah mutlaqah) does not give rise to shuf'ah because it is not a sale. However, a hibah bi shart al-'iwad (gift with a condition of compensation) is treated as a sale in its effect, so shuf'ah is established. The text explains the reasoning: initially it is a gift, so the conditions of gift apply (delivery, and the property must be divided, not shared). Ultimately, it is a sale, so the ruling of sale (including shuf'ah) applies. The passage also contrasts the case where compensation is given later without being stipulated in the contract—then it remains an absolute gift, no shuf'ah, but the compensation prevents the donor from revoking the gift. The commentator references the fuller discussion in the Hidayah's chapter on gifts.

Key Terms
ہبہ (hibah): A gift, a voluntary transfer of ownership without compensation.
ہبہ بشرط العوض (hibah bi shart al-'iwad): A gift conditioned upon the donee giving something in return; treated as a gift initially and a sale ultimately.
شفعہ (shuf'ah): Pre-emption: the right of a co-owner or neighbor to purchase property sold to a third party.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Which of the following is a condition for shuf'ah to apply in a gift with conditioned compensation?
A) The donor must have received the compensation before the gift is delivered.
B) The gifted property and the compensation must both be delivered and must be divided (not undivided).
C) The gift must be absolutely free of any condition.
D) The donee must be a neighbor of the donor.

Key Takeaway
Shuf'ah is established in hibah bi shart al-'iwad because it is a sale in its end, but the contract must still satisfy the gift conditions of delivery and division.

Shuf'ah When Seller Has Option Stipulation (Khiyar al-Shart)
Explaining shuf'ah when the seller retains a conditional option

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on shuf'ah when the seller has a conditional option and apply the requirement of immediate demand upon removal of the option.

Core Idea
No preemption right exists while the seller retains an option; shuf'ah arises only when ownership becomes irrevocable.

This passage explains a key rule of preemption (shuf'ah) in Hanafi fiqh: the right of shuf'ah does not arise until ownership is fully transferred. When a seller stipulates an option (khiyar al-shart), ownership remains with the seller, so the preemptor cannot claim shuf'ah. Once the option is dropped, ownership transfers, and the preemptor must demand shuf'ah immediately.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is selling with a conditional option (khiyar al-shart). The ruling is that there is no shuf'ah for the preemptor during the option period.
The option prevents ownership transfer.
2
Locate the dalil (proof)
The proof is the reasoning (talil): the option prevents transfer of ownership from the seller.
No direct textual proof is cited; the reasoning is based on the legal effect of khiyar.
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah
The sale becomes a complete cause (sabab) for shuf'ah only when the option is removed. Before that, the ownership is not fully transferred, so shuf'ah cannot be claimed.
The connection is that shuf'ah follows ownership transfer.
4
Note the condition
The demand (talab) for shuf'ah must be made at the moment the option is dropped. This is the sahih (correct) view.
Immediate demand is a condition for the right to be established.
5
Evaluate the tarjih
The phrase 'fi al-sahih' indicates that this is the preferred position in the Hanafi school, overruling any view that allows delayed demand.
Tarjih by the text itself.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
بائع کیلئے خیار شرط کے ہوتے ہوئے شفعہ کا حق ملے گا یا نہیں قال ومن باع بشرط الخيار فلا شفعة للشفيع لانه يمنع زوال الملك عن البائع فان اسقط الخيار وجبت الشفعة لانه زال المانع عن الزوال ويشترط الطلب عند سقوط الخيار في الصحيح لان البيع يصير سببا
Will the preemptor get the right of shuf'ah when the seller has a conditional option? He said: And whoever sells with a condition of option, there is no shuf'ah for the preemptor, because it prevents the transfer of ownership from the seller. Then if he drops the option, shuf'ah becomes obligatory, because the preventer of transfer has been removed. And it is condition that the demand be made at the time of dropping the option, in the correct view, because the sale becomes a cause.
Source pages p. 94

Commentary
This passage discusses shuf'ah when the seller has khiyar al-shart (a stipulated option to cancel the sale within a specific period). The ruling is that as long as the option exists, ownership has not fully transferred from the seller, so the preemptor cannot claim shuf'ah. Once the seller drops the option (or it expires), ownership transfers, and the preemptor's right is established. However, the demand must be made at that moment (immediately upon the dropping of the option), because the sale becomes a complete cause for shuf'ah. This is the correct (sahih) position according to the Hanafi school.

Key Terms
خیار شرط (khiyar al-shart): An option stipulated in a sale allowing one party to cancel the sale within a specified period.
شفیع (shafi'): The person entitled to preemption (shuf'ah).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
A seller sells a house with a three-day option to cancel. On the second day, the seller drops the option. When can the preemptor demand shuf'ah?
A) Immediately at the time of the sale contract.
B) Only after the three days have passed.
C) At the moment the option is dropped.
D) The preemptor cannot demand shuf'ah because the option existed.

Key Takeaway
Shuf'ah right arises only when the option is dropped and must be demanded immediately; otherwise, the right may be lost.

The Timing of Pre-emption Demand When the Seller Has an Option
Explains when shuf'ah arises if seller has option, and timing of demand

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state why the pre-emptor's demand (talab) is required after the seller drops his option (khiyar) and apply this ruling to scenarios with options.

Core Idea
Pre-emption is established only when the sale becomes binding—demand before that is futile.

In this passage from Al-Hidayah, we learn a key nuance of pre-emption (shuf'ah): the right to pre-empt does not arise until the sale actually transfers ownership. If the seller has an option (khiyar), the property remains his, so the pre-emptor's claim is premature. The correct timing of the demand (talab) is therefore critical.

Guided Path
1
Understand the effect of seller's option on ownership
Recognize that if the seller has an option, the sold property does not leave his ownership until the option is dropped. This is a fundamental principle from earlier in Hidayah.
Buyer's option has different effects; focus on seller's option here.
2
Connect shuf'ah right to ownership transfer
Shuf'ah is a right to take property upon its sale; it arises only when ownership actually transfers. Therefore, as long as the seller's ownership persists, there is no cause (sabab) for shuf'ah.
The cause is the binding sale that transfers ownership.
3
Identify the correct timing of talab
The demand (talab) must be made after the seller drops the option, because only then does the sale become binding and the cause for shuf'ah exist. Any demand before that is meaningless.
This is the position of Sahib al-Hidayah and the relied-upon view.
4
Distinguish from the minority view
Some earlier scholars held that talab is at the time of the contract. The correct view rejects this because the cause is not yet actualized.
The difference arises from different understandings of 'cause' (sabab).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب الشفعة
لزوال الملك عند ذلك
ترجمة ما مقدوري نے کہا اور جس نے بچا خيار کی شرط کے ساتھ تو شفیع کیلئے شفعہ نہیں ہے اسلئے کہ یہ خیار بائع روکتا ہے بالغ کی ملک کے زوال کو۔ پس اگر بائع نے خیار ساقط کر دیا تو شفعہ ثابت ہو گا اسلئے کہ وہ چیز زائل ہوگئی جو زوال ملک کو روکنے والی تھی اور طلب شرط ہے۔ صحیح قول کے مطابق خیار کے سقوط کے وقت اسلئے کہ بیع سبب بنتی ہے زوال ملک کا اس وقت (سقوط خیار کے وقت)۔ تشریح ہدایہ ص ۱۴ ج ۳ پر آپ پڑھ چکے کہ اگر بائع کو خیار ہو تو مبیع بائع کی ملکیت سے خارج نہ ہوگی۔ اور اگر مشتری کو خیار ہو تو مبیع بائع کی ملکیت سے نکل جائے گی۔ پھر مشتری کی ملکیت میں آئے گی یا نہیں اس میں امام ابو حنیفہ اور صاحبین کا اختلاف ہے۔ بہر حال جب یہ تفصیل معلوم ہے تو اب سنئے، بائع نے کوئی گھر فروخت کیا اور اپنے لئے خیار کی شرط لگالی تو چونکہ مبیع بائع کی ملکیت میں ہے تو ابھی شفیع کو حق شفعہ نہیں پہنچے گا۔ ہاں جب بائع خیار کو ساقط کر دے اور بیع کو نافذ کر دے تو جو چیز مانع تھی وہ زائل ہوگئی اور بیع بائع کی ملکیت سے نکل گئی تو اب شفیع کو شفعہ ملے گا۔ سوال اب رہا یہ مسئلہ کہ شفعہ کی طلب کس وقت شرط ہے؟ جواب تو اس کا جواب یہ ہے کہ اگر چہ بعض مشائخ نے وجود شرط شفعہ کا خیال کرتے ہوئے یوں کہا ہے کہ بوقت بیع طلب شرط ہے۔ مگر صاحب ہدایہ فرماتے ہیں کہ یہ قول صحیح نہیں بلکہ صحیح قول یہ ہے کہ طلب اس وقت شرط ہے جبکہ بائع خیار کو ساقط کر دے۔ کیونکہ جب بائع خیار کو ساقط کر دے گا اس وقت یہ بیع شفعہ کا سبب بنے گی۔ لہذا تحقق سبب سے پہلے طلب لغو ہے۔
Book of Pre-emption. Due to the transfer of ownership at that time. Translation: Ma Quduri said: And whoever buys with a condition of option, there is no right of pre-emption for the pre-emptor, because this option prevents the transfer of ownership from the seller. So if the seller drops the option, then pre-emption is established, because the thing that prevented the transfer of ownership has been removed. And the demand is a condition. According to the correct saying, at the time of the dropping of the option, because the sale becomes a cause for transfer of ownership at that time (the time of dropping the option). Explanation: In Hidayah page 14, volume 3, you have read that if the seller has an option, the sold property does not leave the seller's ownership. And if the buyer has an option, the sold property leaves the seller's ownership. Then whether it enters the buyer's ownership or not, there is a difference between Imam Abu Hanifah and the two companions (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad). Anyway, now that this detail is known, listen: The seller sold a house and stipulated an option for himself. Since the sold property is in the seller's ownership, the pre-emptor will not yet have the right of pre-emption. Yes, when the seller drops the option and makes the sale effective, then the thing that was preventing is removed, and the sale leaves the seller's ownership, so now the pre-emptor gets the right of pre-emption. Question: Now the issue remains: at what time is the demand for pre-emption a condition? Answer: The answer is that although some scholars, considering the existence of the condition of pre-emption, have said that the demand is a condition at the time of the sale, but the author of Hidayah says that this saying is not correct; rather the correct saying is that the demand is a condition at the time when the seller drops the option. Because when the seller drops the option, at that time this sale becomes the cause of pre-emption. Therefore, before the cause is realized, the demand is futile.
Source pages p. 95

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with explanation (sharh) on the pre-emption (shuf'ah) when the seller has a condition of option (khiyar al-ba'i'). The author begins by stating the ruling: if someone buys with an option for the seller, the pre-emptor does not get the right of pre-emption because the option prevents the transfer of ownership from the seller. Once the seller drops the option, pre-emption becomes established. Then the author addresses a debated point: when must the pre-emptor make his demand (talab)? Some earlier scholars held that the demand must occur at the time of the sale itself, but the correct position (as per Sahib al-Hidayah) is that the demand is required only when the seller drops the option. The reasoning is that the sale becomes a cause for pre-emption (sabab) only at that moment; before the cause is actualized, the demand is meaningless. The author also references a previous discussion in Hidayah about the effect of options on ownership: if the seller has an option, the property remains in his ownership; if the buyer has an option, the property leaves the seller's ownership, but whether it enters the buyer's ownership is disputed between Imam Abu Hanifah and the two companions. This background is essential for understanding the timing of the pre-emptor's right.

Key Terms
خیار البائع (khiyar al-ba'i'): An option stipulated by the seller in the sale contract, during which the seller can cancel the sale; ownership does not transfer until option is dropped.
طلب (talab): The demand made by the pre-emptor to assert his right of shuf'ah; it is a condition for the right to be enforceable.
سبب (sabab): The legal cause that gives rise to the right of pre-emption; here it is the binding sale that transfers ownership.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) At the time of the contract of sale
B) When he first learns of the sale
C) After the seller drops his option
D) Within three days of the contract

Key Takeaway
The pre-emptor's demand (talab) is a condition that must be fulfilled after the seller drops the option; only then does the sale become the cause (sabab) for pre-emption.

Pre-emption When the Buyer Has an Option
Ruling with proof and expansion on pre-emption when buyer has option

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi ruling that shuf'ah is valid when the buyer has an option, and explain that the pre-emptor's taking within the option period makes the sale binding and does not transfer the option to the pre-emptor.

Core Idea
Pre-emption is valid despite the buyer's option; once the pre-emptor takes, the sale becomes binding.

This lesson deals with a specific scenario in pre-emption (shuf'ah): the buyer has stipulated a condition of option (khiyar al-mushtari). The Hanafi school rules that the pre-emptor can still exercise his right during the option period, and that his taking renders the sale irrevocable. We will explore the legal reasoning behind this, based on the consensus that the buyer's option does not impede the transfer of ownership from the seller – the very basis of shuf'ah.

Guided Path
1
Identify the scenario
The passage discusses a case where a buyer purchases property with a conditional option (e.g., three days to decide). The seller's ownership transfers, but the buyer can cancel. Identify all parties: seller, buyer with option, pre-emptor.
2
State the ruling on shuf'ah
According to the Hanafi school, shuf'ah is established in this case. The reason is that the buyer's option does not prevent the transfer of ownership from the seller, which is the foundation of pre-emption. The passage cites consensus (ittifaq).
The seller's option would prevent transfer; buyer's option does not.
3
Recognize the effect of the pre-emptor's taking
If the pre-emptor takes the property within the three-day option period, the sale becomes binding (lazim). The buyer loses his ability to reject the sale. The pre-emptor does not inherit the buyer's option.
4
Understand why the option does not pass to the pre-emptor
The option is a contractual right agreed between seller and buyer. The pre-emptor is a third party; the contract of shuf'ah is separate. The option does not transfer to the pre-emptor.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مشتری کیلئے خیار شرط کے ساتھ خریدی ہوئی زمین میں شفعہ ہے
وان اشترى بشرط الخيار وجبت الشفعة لانه لا يمنع زوال الملك عن البائع بالاتفاق والشفعة تبتنى عليه علی مامر واذا اخذها فى الثلث وجب البيع لعجز المشترى عن الرد ولاخيار للشفيع لانه يثبت بالشرط وهو للمشترى دون الشفيع
ترجمہ اور اگر مشتری نے خیار کی شرط کے ساتھ خریدا تو شفعہ ثابت ہوگا۔ اسلئے کہ یہ بالاتفاق بائع سے ملک کے زوال کو نہیں روکتا اور شفعہ گذشتہ تفصیل کے مطابق اسی پر مبنی ہے اور جبکہ شفیع نے گھر کو تین دن کے اندر اندر لے لیا تو بیع لازم ہوگئی مشتری کے رد سے عاجز ہو جانے کی وجہ سے اور شفیع کیلئے خیار نہیں ہے۔ اسلئے کہ خیار شرط سے ثابت ہوتا ہے اور یہ مشتری کیلئے ہے نہ کہ شفیع کیلئے۔
تشریح پہلے مسئلہ میں خیار بائع کا ذکر تھا اور اس میں مشتری کے خیار کا ذکر ہے اور ماقبل میں معلوم ہو چکا ہے کہ مشتری کے خیار کی صورت میں مبیع بائع کی ملکیت سے نکل جاتی ہے۔ معلوم ہوا کہ اس صورت میں شفعہ ملے گا۔ کیونکہ شفعہ کا مدار بائع کی ملکیت کے زوال پر ہے۔ بہر حال جب مشتری نے اپنے لئے خیار لیا تو اس کو اختیار ہے کہ تین دن کے اندر اندر چاہے بیع کو رد کر دے اور چاہے بیع کو لازم و نافذ کر دے لیکن اگر تین دن کے اندر اندر شفیع اس مکان کو شفعہ میں لے لے تو اب بیع خود بخود لازم ہوگئی کیونکہ اب مشتری بیع کے رد کرنے پر قادر نہیں رہا۔ اب اس پر سوال ہوتا ہے کہ جو خیار مشتری کیلئے ہے یہ شفیع کے واسطے بھی ہوگا یا نہیں تو جواب دیا کہ نہیں کیونکہ خیار شرط، شرط لگانے سے ملتا ہے اور یہ معاہدہ بائع اور مشتری کے درمیان ہوا ہے نہ کہ بائع اور شفیع کے درمیان۔
There is pre-emption in land bought by the buyer with a condition of option. And if he buys with a condition of option, pre-emption is established because it does not prevent transfer of ownership from the seller by agreement, and pre-emption is based on that, as has passed. And if he takes it within three days, the sale becomes binding due to the buyer's inability to return it, and there is no option for the pre-emptor because it is established by the condition and it is for the buyer, not for the pre-emptor. Translation: And if the buyer bought with a condition of option, pre-emption is established. Because it does not prevent transfer of ownership from the seller by consensus, and pre-emption is based on that, as per the previous detail. And when the pre-emptor took the house within three days, the sale became binding due to the buyer's inability to return it, and there is no option for the pre-emptor. Because the option is established by the condition and it is for the buyer, not for the pre-emptor. Explanation: In the first issue, the seller's option was mentioned, and here the buyer's option is mentioned. And it has been learned earlier that in the case of the buyer's option, the sold property leaves the seller's ownership. So it is understood that in this case, pre-emption will be given. Because the basis of pre-emption is the transfer of ownership from the seller. Anyway, when the buyer took an option for himself, he has the choice to reject the sale or make it binding within three days. But if within three days the pre-emptor takes the property through pre-emption, then the sale automatically becomes binding because the buyer is no longer able to reject it. Now the question arises: does the option that is for the buyer also apply to the pre-emptor? The answer is no, because the conditional option is given by stipulation, and this contract was made between the seller and the buyer, not between the seller and the pre-emptor.
Source pages p. 95

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with explanation on pre-emption when the buyer has a condition of option (khiyar al-mushtari). The author first states the ruling: if the buyer purchases with an option, pre-emption is established because the buyer's option does not prevent the transfer of ownership from the seller (by consensus). This is different from the seller's option, which does prevent transfer. Pre-emption is based on the transfer of ownership from the seller. The author then explains the effect if the pre-emptor takes the property within the three-day option period: the sale becomes binding (lazim), and the buyer loses his ability to reject the sale. Additionally, the pre-emptor does not acquire the option (khiyar) that the buyer had, because the option was stipulated for the buyer alone, not for the pre-emptor. The reasoning is that the option is a contractual right agreed between the seller and buyer; it does not extend to a third party. The commentary also clarifies that the basis for shuf'ah is the transfer of ownership from the seller, which occurs in this case, so pre-emption is valid.

Key Terms
خیار المشتری (khiyar al-mushtari): An option stipulated by the buyer in the sale contract, allowing him to cancel the sale within a specified period (usually three days).
شُفْعَة (shuf'ah): The right of pre-emption that allows a co-owner or neighbor to purchase a sold property by paying the buyer the price.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
When the buyer has a condition of option, and the pre-emptor takes the property within three days, what happens to the sale?
A) The sale is void
B) The sale becomes binding and the buyer loses his option to reject
C) The pre-emptor gets the buyer's option
D) The seller can cancel the sale

Key Takeaway
Shuf'ah is valid when the buyer has an option because ownership transfers; the pre-emptor's taking within the option period makes the sale binding and does not transfer the option to the pre-emptor.

Pre-emption for Buyer with Option: The Ishkal and Its Hanafi Resolution
Ruling on shuf'ah for option‑holder with ishkal resolution

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why the buyer with an option can claim pre‑emption according to the relied‑upon Hanafi position, and identify the ishkal according to Imam Abu Hanifah.

Core Idea
The buyer's pre‑emption action implies acceptance of the sale, thereby establishing ownership.

A key scenario in pre‑emption law arises when the buyer of an adjacent property still holds an option from his own purchase. Can he pre‑empt another property sold next to his? The Hanafi school says yes, but this ruling faced a difficulty (ishkal) based on Imam Abu Hanifah’s view that ownership does not transfer to the buyer during the option period. This lesson unpacks that ishkal and its resolution, showing how Hanafi jurists harmonised the ruling with the principle that pre‑emption requires ownership.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ruling and its immediate context
The passage states that if a house adjacent to the pre-emptor’s property is sold, and either the seller or the buyer of that house has an option, the option-holder may pre‑empt. For the seller it is straightforward. For the buyer, the text notes an ishkal.
2
Pinpoint the ishkal and its source
The ishkal arises from Imam Abu Hanifah's view: during the option period, the property leaves the seller's ownership but does not enter the buyer's ownership. Pre‑emption is based on ownership, so how can the buyer pre‑empt? The two companions (Sahibayn) hold that ownership does transfer, so there is no problem for them.
The ishkal is purely internal to the Hanafi school.
3
Understand the resolution
The resolution is that the buyer’s act of taking the property by pre‑emption constitutes an implied acceptance (ijazah) of his own sale. This drops his option and makes the sale binding, thereby establishing his ownership at that very moment. Thus the ownership condition for pre‑emption is satisfied.
This resolution reconciles the ruling with Imam Abu Hanifah’s principle.
4
Distinguish from the option of inspection
The passage notes that if the buyer has the option of inspection (khiyar al‑ru'yah), taking by pre‑emption does NOT invalidate it. That option is even stronger – it cannot be annulled even explicitly, so it certainly cannot be annulled by implication. This underlines the uniqueness of khiyar al‑ru'yah.

And if a house adjacent to it is sold and the option is for one of them, then he has the right to take it by pre-emption. As for the seller, it is obvious because his ownership remains in the house by which he is pre-empting. Similarly when it is for the buyer, and there is an issue (ishkal) which we have clarified in the Book of Sales, so we will not repeat it. And when he (the buyer) takes it, it is an acceptance of the sale on his part, unlike the case when he bought it and had not seen it, where his option is not invalidated by taking by pre-emption the house sold adjacent to it, because the option of inspection is not invalidated by explicit annulment, so how could it be by implication? Then, when the pre-emptor of the first house appears, he has the right to take it, not the second, because his ownership in the first was non-existent when the second was sold. Anyway, for the seller it is obvious because of the remaining of his ownership in the house by which he is pre-empting. And similarly when it is for the buyer, and there is an issue (ishkal) which we have clarified in the Book of Sales, so we will not repeat it. And when the buyer takes this house, it is an acceptance of the sale on his part. Unlike the case when the buyer bought it and had not seen it, in the sense that his option is not invalidated by taking by pre-emption the house sold adjacent to it. Because the option of inspection is not invalidated by explicit annulment, so how could it be by implication? Then, when the pre-emptor of the first house came, he has the right to take it, not the second, because his ownership in the first was non-existent when the second was sold. Explanation: 'And if a house...' etc. From here, they state the second issue: if a house adjacent to a house is being sold, and there is an option for the seller or the buyer, do they have the right of pre-emption? He said: whichever of them has the option, he will get pre-emption. As for why the seller gets it, the answer is obvious: when the option is for the seller, the house is in his ownership, so he gets pre-emption. And if the option is for the buyer, he also gets pre-emption. Regarding this, Sahib al-Hidayah says: 'And there is an issue which we have clarified in the Book of Sales, so we will not repeat it.' The issue is that this is correct according to the two companions (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) because in their view the house is owned by the buyer. But according to Imam Abu Hanifah, although the house leaves the seller's ownership, it does not enter the buyer's ownership, and pre-emption is based on ownership. So why did the pre-emptor get it? This is the issue. But the answer is that when he (the pre-emptor) proceeded to take by pre-emption, this action is evidence that he (the buyer) dropped his option and made the sale binding, and now his ownership is apparent. And the author did not mention this issue and answer in Hidayah; perhaps it was mentioned in Kifayat al-Muntaha.
Source pages p. 95

Commentary
This passage deals with a scenario where a property adjacent to another is sold, and either the seller or the buyer of that adjacent property has an option. The ruling is that the one with the option can claim pre-emption. For the seller, it is straightforward because he still owns the property he is using to claim pre-emption. For the buyer, there is an ishkal (difficulty) according to Imam Abu Hanifah. According to the two companions (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad), the buyer owns the property during the option period, so pre-emption based on ownership is valid. But in Imam Abu Hanifah's view, although the property leaves the seller's ownership, it does not enter the buyer's ownership until the option is dropped or the period expires. Since pre-emption is based on ownership, how can the buyer claim pre-emption? The author (al-Marghinani) says he has clarified this ishkal in Kitab al-Buyu' and does not repeat it here. The commentator (Ashraf al-Hidayah) then explains the resolution: when the buyer (who has the option) takes the adjacent property by pre-emption, his action constitutes an acceptance (ijazah) of the sale, effectively dropping his option and making the sale binding. Thus, at that moment, ownership is established, and pre-emption is valid. The passage also distinguishes this from the option of inspection (khiyar al-ru'yah): taking a property by pre-emption does not invalidate the option of inspection because that option is even stronger than the conditional option—it is not invalidated even by explicit annulment, let alone by implication. Finally, a rule is given: if a pre-emptor of a first property appears, he may pre-empt that first property but not the second one sold adjacent to his own, because at the time the second property was sold, he did not own the first property (it had been sold earlier, so his ownership was nonexistent).

Key Terms
خیار الرؤیة (khiyar al-ru'yah): The option of inspection: the buyer has the right to cancel the sale after seeing the property if it does not meet expectations.
إجازة (ijazah): Acceptance or ratification of a contract; here, the buyer's action of taking by pre-emption is considered an acceptance of his own sale, making it binding.
إشكال (ishkal): A difficulty or problem in the ruling; here it refers to the inconsistency between Imam Abu Hanifah's view on ownership and the ruling that the buyer can pre-empt.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the relied‑upon (mufta bihi) Hanafi position, when a buyer with a conditional option pre‑empts an adjacent property, what legal effect does his pre‑emption action have on his original sale contract?
A) It cancels the original sale.
B) It constitutes an acceptance of the original sale, making it binding.
C) It has no effect on the original sale.
D) It extends the option period.

Key Takeaway
The buyer with an option can pre‑empt an adjacent property; the apparent conflict with Imam Abu Hanifah’s view on ownership is resolved by treating the pre‑emption as an implied acceptance of the original sale.

No Shuf'a in Voidable Sale (Bay' Fasid)
Ruling on shuf'a in voidable sale

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling that shuf'a is not established in a voidable sale and explain the reasoning that granting shuf'a would confirm the defect.

Core Idea
Shuf'a in a voidable sale would confirm the defect, contradicting Sharia's command to annul it.

Pre-emption (shuf'a) is a right that arises from a valid sale. But what if the sale itself is defective? The Hanafi school holds that shuf'a does not apply to a voidable sale (bay' fasid), whether before or after possession. This lesson unpacks the reasoning behind this ruling and distinguishes it from valid sales where the buyer has an option.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ruling and its scope
State the Hanafi position: no shuf'a in a voidable sale. Note the two scenarios: before possession (seller still owner) and after possession (possibility of annulment).
2
Analyze the reasoning for each scenario
Before possession: the seller's ownership has not ceased. After possession: shuf'a would confirm the defect, contradicting the Sharia command to annul the sale to remove the defect.
The key principle: Sharia commands annulment to remove defect; shuf'a would confirm it, so it is prohibited.
3
Contrast with a valid sale with khiyar
In a valid sale with an option for the buyer, shuf'a is allowed because the buyer has full authority to transact (including waiving the option), unlike in a voidable sale where the buyer is prohibited from transacting.
This distinction answers the objection that both involve possibility of annulment.
4
Evaluate the objection and resolution
Objection: If possibility of annulment bars shuf'a in voidable sale, why not in a valid sale with option? Answer: In a valid sale, the buyer can still transact; in a voidable sale, he is legally prevented.
This is a qiyas khafi (subtle analogy) that is distinguished by the difference in transactional authority.

Source Text

اسلئے کہ یہاں دو ہی صورتیں ہیں یا تو مشتری قبضہ کر چکا ہے یا نہیں ۔ اگر قبضہ نہیں کیا تو ابھی وہ گھر بائع کی ملکیت میں ہے جہاں ماقبل میں ذکر کردہ اصول کے مطابق شفعہ ملے گا ہی نہیں ۔ اور اگر مشری قابض ہو چکا ہے اب بھی شفعہ نہیں ملے گا کیونکہ شریعت نے یہاں یہ حکم دیا ہے کہ اس بیچ کو فسخ کرو تا کہ فساد بیج کا شر دور ہو سکے اور اگر شفعہ دے دیا تو فساد بجائے دور ہونے کے پختہ ہو جائے گا اس وجہ سے شفعہ نہیں ملے گا۔ بخلاف ما اذا .. الخ یہاں سے ایک اعتراض و جواب کا ذکر ہے۔ اعتراض یہ ہے کہ جس طرح بیع فاسد کے اندر بیع کے فسخ اور ٹوٹ جانے کا احتمال ہے اسی طرح بیع صحیح کے اندر جب مشتری کیلئے خیار ہو تب بھی یہی احتمال ہے کہ وہ بیع کو توڑ دے اور رد کر دے تو جس طرح شراء فاسد کی صورت میں شفعہ نہیں دیا گیا مشتری کے خیار کی صورت میں بھی شفعہ نہ ملنا چاہئے؟ جواب تو اس کا جواب دیا کہ ان دونوں میں زمین و آسمان کا فرق ہے کیونکہ بیع صحیح میں مشتری کو بیع میں تصرف کرنے کا اختیار ہے اور بیع فاسد کے اندر مشتری کو مبیع میں تصرف کرنے کا اختیار نہیں ہے بلکہ شریعت نے مشتری کو تصرف کرنے سے روک دیا ہے۔ فلا اشکال فیہ،
And when he takes it... etc. meaning when the buyer takes the house via shuf'a, this becomes permission for the sale from his side. Contrary to... etc. Yes, if the buyer took the house via shuf'a and he had not seen the house. Now he sees it, so he will have the option of inspection, and taking the house via shuf'a does not void his option of inspection. Because the option of inspection is not voided even by explicit annulment, so how can it be voided by implication of annulment? Then when the shafi' comes... etc. The buyer took the house via shuf'a, now the shafi' of the first house comes and claims shuf'a. He will only get shuf'a in the first house, not the second, because at the time the second house was sold, the ownership of the shafi' was non-existent, so he cannot get the second house via shuf'a. Section: The ruling for the shafi' in a house sold through a voidable sale. Imam Quduri said: 'Whoever buys a house with a voidable purchase, there is no shuf'a in it. Before possession, because the seller's ownership has not ceased; after possession, because of the possibility of annulment, and the right of annulment is established by Sharia to remove the defect. And in establishing the right of shuf'a is confirmation of the defect, so it is not permissible. Contrary to when the buyer has an option in a valid sale, because he becomes specifically entitled to transact in it, whereas in a voidable sale he is prohibited from transacting.' Translation: Imam Quduri said: 'And whoever buys a house with a voidable purchase, there is no shuf'a in it. In any case, before possession due to the seller's ownership not having ceased, and after possession due to the possibility of annulment, and the right of annulment is established by Sharia to remove the defect. And in establishing the right of shuf'a is confirming the defect, so it is not permissible. Contrary to when the buyer has an option in a valid sale, because the buyer is specifically entitled to transact in it, whereas in a voidable sale he is prevented from transacting.' Explanation: The detailed discussion of voidable sale has already passed in Volume 3 of al-Hidayah. In any case, if someone sold his house but the sale became voidable due to a void condition, then will the shafi' get shuf'a? The answer is no. Why? Because there are two scenarios: either the buyer has taken possession or not. If he has not taken possession, the house is still in the seller's ownership, so shuf'a is not given as per the established principle. And if the buyer has taken possession, still shuf'a is not given because Sharia has commanded that this sale be annulled to remove the defect, and if shuf'a is given, the defect would be confirmed instead of removed. Therefore shuf'a is not given. Contrary to the case when... etc. From here an objection and answer are mentioned. The objection is: just as in a voidable sale there is a possibility of annulment, similarly in a valid sale when the buyer has an option there is also a possibility that he may annul and reject the sale. So just as shuf'a is not given in the case of a voidable purchase, it should also not be given in the case of the buyer's option. The answer is: there is a vast difference between the two because in a valid sale, the buyer has the right to transact in the sale, whereas in a voidable sale, the buyer is prohibited from transacting in the sold item. So there is no objection.
Source pages p. 96

Commentary
This composite passage covers three sub-topics: (1) reiteration that taking shuf'a does not void khiyar al-ru'ya; (2) a scenario where the shafi' of the first house appears after the buyer has taken the second house via shuf'a; and (3) the detailed ruling on shuf'a in a voidable sale (bay' fasid). The main focus is the third part, introduced with a quote from Imam Quduri: there is no shuf'a in a voidable sale. The reasons are twofold: before possession, the seller still owns the property; after possession, the sale is subject to annulment to eliminate the defect, and granting shuf'a would confirm the defect. This is contrasted with a valid sale where the buyer has an option (khiyar), in which shuf'a is given because the buyer can transact freely. An objection is raised: if the possibility of annulment in a voidable sale bars shuf'a, then why doesn't the possibility of annulment in a valid sale with an option also bar shuf'a? The answer is that in a valid sale, the buyer has full authority to transact (including waiving his option), whereas in a voidable sale, the buyer is legally prevented from transacting. Therefore, the analogy fails, and shuf'a is allowed in valid sales with options but not in voidable sales.

Key Terms
بیع فاسد (bay' fasid): A sale that is defective due to a condition or element that violates Sharia but can be rectified; it is voidable.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why is shuf'a not given in a voidable sale even after the buyer has taken possession?
A) Because the seller still owns the property.
B) Because granting shuf'a would confirm the defective sale, contradicting the Sharia command to annul it.
C) Because the buyer never becomes owner.
D) Because the property is considered unsold.

Key Takeaway
Shuf'a is not established in a voidable sale because granting it would confirm the defect, contradicting the Sharia command to annul such a sale.

Khiyar al-Ru'yah Not Invalidated by Shuf'ah
Ruling on khiyar al-ru'yah and shuf'ah interaction

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state that khiyar al-ru'yah is not invalidated by acquiring a property via shuf'ah and determine the scope of shuf'ah when the buyer has previously acquired property via shuf'ah.

Core Idea
Khiyar al-ru'yah endures even after shuf'ah; a shafi' only gets the first house, not the one acquired via shuf'ah.

This passage from the Hidayah commentary addresses two related rulings when khiyar al-ru'yah and shuf'ah intersect. The first affirms the strength of khiyar al-ru'yah: it is not invalidated by the buyer's subsequent acquisition of the property via shuf'ah. The second clarifies that when a shafi' claims shuf'ah on an original sale, his preemptive right does not extend to a second property that the buyer later acquired via shuf'ah. The reasoning hinges on the nature of khiyar al-ru'yah as a robust right and the condition that shuf'ah requires the shafi' to have had ownership interest at the time of the sale.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two rulings
Extract the two legal conclusions from the passage: (1) khiyar al-ru'yah remains valid after shuf'ah; (2) the shafi' of the first house only gets shuf'ah in that house, not in the second house bought via shuf'ah.
Both rulings are explicitly stated in the text.
2
Analyze the reasoning for the first ruling
Understand why khiyar al-ru'yah is not invalidated: it is such a strong right that even explicit waiver does not invalidate it. Therefore, an implicit action like taking the house via shuf'ah is insufficient to invalidate it.
The author uses a qiyas argument: since explicit invalidation fails, implicit is even weaker.
3
Analyze the reasoning for the second ruling
Recognize that the shafi' only has right to the first house because he had ownership in that property at the time of its sale, but he had no ownership in the second house when it was sold. Shuf'ah is tied to the property in which the claimant had a share.
The shafi' never owned the second house, so his right does not extend to it.
4
Apply the principles
Test a hypothetical: A buys house X with khiyar al-ru'yah. Later, A acquires house Y via shuf'ah. Then a neighbor of X claims shuf'ah on X. He gets X only, not Y. Also, even though A bought Y via shuf'ah, his khiyar on X remains valid.
This scenario matches the passage exactly.

Source Text

خیار رویت ملے گا اور شفعہ میں مکان لینے کی وجہ سے اس کا خیار رویت باطل نہیں ہوتا۔ کیونکہ خیار رویت تو صراحتا باطل کرنے سے بھی باطل نہیں ہوتا اور شفعہ میں مکان لینا تو ابطال خیار رویت کی صرف دلیل ہے تو جو حق صراحة باطل کرنے سے باطل نہیں ہوتا وہ ابطال کی دلالت سے کیسے باطل ہو جائے گا۔ ثم اذا حضر .. الخ مشتری نے شفعہ میں مکان لے لیا اب پہلے گھر کے شفیع نے آکر شفعہ کا دعوی کیا تو اس کو صرف پہلے گھر میں شفعہ ملے گا۔ دوسرے میں نہیں کیونکہ جس وقت دوسرا مکان فروخت ہوا اس وقت شفیع کی ملکیت معدوم تھی تو اس کو دوسرا گھر شفعہ میں نہیں مل سکے گا۔
Khiyar al-ru'yah will be given, and due to taking the house via shuf'ah, his khiyar al-ru'yah does not become invalid. Because khiyar al-ru'yah is not invalidated even by explicitly invalidating it, and taking the house via shuf'ah is only evidence of invalidating khiyar al-ru'yah, so how can a right that is not invalidated by explicit invalidation become invalidated by implication? Then when he attends... etc. The buyer took the house via shuf'ah, now the shafi' of the first house came and claimed shuf'ah, so he will only get shuf'ah in the first house, not in the second, because at the time the second house was sold, the shafi''s ownership was extinct, so he cannot get the second house via shuf'ah.
Source pages p. 97

Commentary
This passage presents two rulings. First, if a buyer acquires a house through shuf'ah (preemption), his existing khiyar al-ru'yah (option of inspection) is not invalidated. The reasoning is that khiyar al-ru'yah is a strong right that remains even if explicitly waived; taking the house via shuf'ah is merely a circumstantial indication of satisfaction, not a direct waiver. Second, consider a scenario where a person buys a house (possibly via shuf'ah) and later another shafi' of the first house (a neighbor or co-owner) appears and claims shuf'ah on the first house after it was sold. The shafi' only gets the first house, not the second house that the buyer acquired via shuf'ah. This is because at the time the second house was sold, the shafi''s ownership in the second house did not exist (he never owned it), so his right of shuf'ah does not extend to it. The passage is commentary expanding on a matn reference (ثم اذا حضر).

Key Terms
خیار رویت (khiyar al-ru'yah): The buyer's option to rescind a sale after seeing the purchased item if it does not meet expectations.
شفعہ (shuf'ah): The right of a co-owner or neighbor to acquire a sold property by compulsory purchase.
شفیع (shafi'): The person entitled to exercise the right of preemption.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the two-house scenario, why does the shafi' of the first house not get the second house via shuf'ah?
A) Because the buyer already owns it via shuf'ah, so it's not subject to another shuf'ah.
B) Because the shafi' had no ownership in the second house at the time it was sold.
C) Because shuf'ah cannot be claimed on a property that was itself acquired via shuf'ah.
D) Because the second house was sold before the first house.

Key Takeaway
Khiyar al-ru'yah is a uniquely strong right that remains even after shuf'ah; shuf'ah is confined to properties in which the claimant had a prior property interest.

No Shuf'ah in Fasid (Defective) Sale
Ruling and reasoning: preemption is not established in a fasid sale

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling that shuf'ah is not established in a fasid sale, explain the reasoning based on the prohibition of confirming a defect, and distinguish between a fasid sale and a valid sale with khiyar.

Core Idea
Granting shuf'ah in a fasid sale would confirm a defect that shari'ah commands to remove.

This passage from al-Quduri's matn and its commentary establishes that preemption (shuf'ah) does not apply to a defective (fasid) sale. The key insight is that shuf'ah would reinforce the defect, whereas Islamic law requires rescission to eliminate the defect. The ruling is consistent before and after possession. The passage also addresses an important distinction: shuf'ah is allowed in a valid sale even when the buyer has an option (khiyar), because in that case the buyer has full disposal authority, unlike in a fasid sale.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Ruling
State the explicit ruling: there is no shuf'ah in a fasid sale.
This is the direct answer from the matn.
2
Analyze the Two Scenarios
Consider before possession (seller still owns) and after possession (shari'ah commands rescission).
Possession does not change the ruling because the defect remains.
3
Understand the Underlying Principle
Shuf'ah would confirm the defect; shari'ah wants the defect removed via faskh.
Thus, shuf'ah contradicts the purpose of shari'ah in fasid contracts.
4
Compare with the Khiyar Scenario
Objection: why allow shuf'ah in a valid sale with khiyar? Distinguish: in valid sale, buyer has disposal right; in fasid sale, buyer is prohibited from disposal.
The presence of disposal authority changes the thamarah.
5
Derive the Thamarah al-Khilaf
In practice, shuf'ah is given in valid sale with khiyar but not in fasid sale.
This is the practical consequence of the distinction.

He said: And whoever buys a house with a fasid (defective) purchase, there is no shuf'ah in it, whether before possession because the seller's ownership has not ceased, or after possession due to the possibility of rescission (faskh). And the right of rescission is established by shari'ah to remove the defect, and in establishing the right of shuf'ah there is confirmation of the defect, so it is not permissible. Contrary to the case when the buyer has an option (khiyar) in a valid sale, because he becomes specifically entitled to dispose of it, whereas in a fasid sale he is prohibited from disposing. Translation: Imam al-Quduri said: And whoever buys a house with a fasid purchase, there is no shuf'ah in it. Regardless, before possession because the seller's ownership has not ceased, and after possession due to the possibility of faskh, and the right of faskh is established by shari'ah to remove the defect. And in establishing the right of shuf'ah, the defect is confirmed, so taking it via shuf'ah is not permissible. Contrary to when the buyer has khiyar in a valid sale, because the buyer is specifically associated with the sold property in terms of disposal, whereas in a fasid sale the buyer is prevented from disposing. Explanation: The detailed discussion of fasid sale has passed in volume 3 of al-Hidayah. In any case, if someone sold his house but due to a defective condition it became a fasid sale, then does the shafi' have shuf'ah or not? The answer is no. Why? Because there are two scenarios: either the buyer has taken possession or not. If possession was not taken, then the house is still in the seller's ownership, and according to the previously mentioned principle, shuf'ah is not granted. And if the buyer has taken possession, still shuf'ah will not be granted, because shari'ah has commanded that this sale be rescinded so that the defect of the sale may be removed, and if shuf'ah is given, the defect will become firm instead of being removed. Therefore, shuf'ah is not given. Contrary to the case when... etc. Here an objection and answer are mentioned. The objection is: Just as in a fasid sale there is a possibility of faskh and dissolution, similarly in a valid sale when the buyer has an option, there is also the possibility that he may break and return the sale, so just as in the case of fasid sale shuf'ah is not given, in the case of khiyar also shuf'ah should not be given? The answer: There is a vast difference between the two, because in a valid sale the buyer has the authority to dispose of the sold property, while in a fasid sale the buyer has no authority to dispose; rather, shari'ah has prevented the buyer from disposing. So there is no difficulty.
Source pages p. 97

Commentary
This passage provides the ruling and reasoning for why there is no shuf'ah (preemption) in a fasid (defective) sale. The primary reason is to prevent the confirmation of a defective contract; shari'ah commands rescission (faskh) of the fasid sale, and granting shuf'ah would effectively confirm the defect. The ruling applies both before and after possession: before possession, the seller still owns the property (so no shuf'ah), and after possession, the possibility of faskh prevents shuf'ah. The passage then addresses a potential objection: in a valid sale with khiyar (option), there is also a possibility of rescission, so why is shuf'ah allowed there? The answer distinguishes the two: in a valid sale, the buyer has full right to dispose (including selling again), whereas in a fasid sale, the buyer is prohibited from disposing. Thus, shuf'ah in a valid sale with khiyar does not conflict with shari'ah's purpose, while in a fasid sale it does. The authority of this ruling is from the matn of al-Quduri (referenced as 'Imam Quduri said') and is standard Hanafi position.

Key Terms
شراء فاسد (shira' fasid): A defective purchase contract that is invalid due to a condition (shart fasid) but can be validated by removing the condition.
فسخ (faskh): Rescission or annulment of a contract, here due to defect.
خیار (khiyar): An option or right to rescind a sale within a specified period.

Key Terms from Source Passage

C) Because granting shuf'ah would confirm the defect, whereas shari'ah commands rescission.
D) Because the buyer is considered a trespasser.

Key Takeaway
Shuf'ah is not established in a fasid sale because granting it would confirm a defect that shari'ah commands to remove, unlike in a valid sale with khiyar where the buyer has disposal authority.

Shuf'ah When Faskh Right Falls and Seller's Preemption
Rulings on shuf'ah when rescission right ends, and seller's shuf'ah

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state when shuf'ah becomes wajib in a fasid sale and explain the effect of the seller's possession and timing relative to the judge's ruling on shuf'ah claims.

Core Idea
Shuf'ah becomes wajib when the right of faskh falls; the seller's ownership at the time of judgment determines his shuf'ah claim.

This passage from al-Hidayah discusses two critical scenarios in shuf'ah law: the removal of the impediment of faskh in a fasid sale, and the preemption rights of the seller when the sold property remains in his possession. The timing of the judge's ruling relative to the transfer of ownership is crucial: ownership after the judgment is not required for a valid shuf'ah claim.

Guided Path
1
Identify the impediment to shuf'ah in a fasid sale.
In a fasid (defective) sale, the buyer has the right to rescind (faskh). This right is an impediment (mani') to the shafi''s claim because ownership is not final. Recognize that as long as faskh is possible, shuf'ah cannot be established.
2
Determine the condition that removes the impediment.
The impediment is removed when the right of faskh falls, i.e., when it becomes impossible to exercise, such as when the buyer resells the property to a third party. At that point, shuf'ah becomes wajib.
The text states: 'إذا سقط حق الفسخ وجبت الشفعة لزوال المانع'
3
Analyze the seller's shuf'ah claim when the sold property is in his possession.
In a fasid sale, if the sold property (mabi') is still in the seller's possession (because delivery hasn't occurred), and an adjacent property is sold, the seller has the right of shuf'ah because he is still the owner. If he has already delivered the mabi', the buyer becomes the owner and thus the shafi'.
The passage distinguishes between the seller's possession and delivery.
4
Evaluate the effect of the timing of the judge's ruling on shuf'ah.
If the seller hands over the mabi' or recovers it from the buyer before the judge rules on his shuf'ah claim, his claim becomes void because his ownership of the property that was the basis of the claim ceases. However, if the transfer occurs after the judgment, his claim remains valid because continuity of ownership after judgment is not a condition.
This is a key point: the shafi''s ownership at the time of judgment is what matters, not after.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال فان سقط حق الفسخ وجبت الشفعة لزوال المانع وان بيعت دار بجنبها وهي في يد البائع بعد فله الشفعة لبقاء ملكه وان سلمها الى المشترى فهو شفيعها لان الملك له ثم ان سلم البائع قبل الحكم بالشفعة له بطلت شفعته كما اذا باع بخلاف ما اذا سلم بعده لان بقاء ملكه فى الدار التي يشفع بها بعد الحكم بالشفعة ليس بشرط فبقيت الماخوذة بالشفعة على ملكه وان استردها البائع من المشترى قبل الحكم بالشفعة له بطلت لا نقطاع گیا کوئی گھر اس کے پہلو میں حالانکہ یہ گھر ابھی تک بائع کے قبضہ میں ہے تو اس کیلئے شفعہ ہے اس کی ملکیت کے باقی رہنے کی وجہ سے اور اگر بائع نے اس کو مشتری کی جانب سپر د کر دیا تو مشتری ہی اس کا شفیع ہو گا۔ اس لئے کہ ملکیت اس کی ہے۔ پھر اگر بائع نے گھر سپر د کر دیا اپنے لئے شفعہ کے حکم سے پہلے ہی تو اس کا شفعہ باطل ہو گیا جیسا کہ جبکہ اس نے بیچ دیا ہو۔ بخلاف اس صورت کے جبکہ اس کے بعد سپر د کر دیا ہو اسلئے کہ شفیع کی ملکیت کا باقی رہنا اس گھر کے اندر جس کے ذریعہ وہ شفعہ طلب کر رہا ہے شفعہ کے حکم کے بعد شرط نہیں ہوتی جو گھر شفعہ میں لیا گیا ہے وہ اس کی ملکیت پر باقی رہے گا۔ اور اگر بائع نے گھر کو مشتری سے واپس کر لیا۔ مشتری کیلئے شفعہ کے حکم سے پہلے تو شفعہ باطل ہو جائے گا۔ مشتری کی ملکیت سے منقطع ہو جانے کی وجہ سے اس گھر سے جس کے ذریعہ وہ شفعہ طلب کر رہا ہے شفعہ کے حکم سے پہلے اور اگر بائع نے He said: Then if the right of faskh falls, shuf'ah becomes obligatory (wajib) due to the removal of the impediment. And if a house is sold adjacent to it while the house is still in the seller's possession, then he has shuf'ah due to the continuation of his ownership. And if he hands it over to the buyer, then the buyer is his shafi' because ownership belongs to him. Then if the seller hands it over before the judgment of shuf'ah for him, his shuf'ah becomes void, just as if he had sold it. Contrary to if he hands it over after [judgment], because the continuation of his ownership in the house by which he claims shuf'ah is not a condition after the judgment of shuf'ah; thus the house taken by shuf'ah remains in his ownership. And if the seller recovers the house from the buyer before the judgment of shuf'ah for him, it becomes void due to the severance of his ownership of the house by which he claims shuf'ah before the judgment of shuf'ah. And if he recovers it after the judgment, the second house remains in his ownership, for the reason we have stated. And if the possibility of faskh has ended, for example, if the buyer sold the house to someone else, then the shafi' will get shuf'ah because the impediment has been removed. Yes, if the seller had already handed it over to the buyer, then shuf'ah belongs to the buyer because now the buyer is the owner. Second case: The sold property was in the seller's possession, and a house adjacent to it was sold, and the seller claimed shuf'ah, but the judge had not yet decided in favor of the seller when the seller handed over the sold property to the buyer, then the seller will not get shuf'ah. As when he sold [the adjacent house?] – this case is exactly like when a person claimed shuf'ah, but before the judge decided in his favor, he sold his own house by virtue of which he was claiming shuf'ah, then his shuf'ah becomes void.
